Preface 
This project has been conducted during 2015-2016 at the Research Group for Diagnostic 
Engineering at the National Food Institute at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The 
project is a final master thesis in the Flexible Master education at DTU in Food Technology and the 
project accounts for 15 ECTS points.  
This project is part of the 4 years research project “METAGENOME KIT: An advanced laboratory 
and software solution for end-to-end diagnostic and typing of foodborne pathogenic gut bacteria 
(HTF File no 85-2013-1) funded by the Innovationsfonden (Højteknologifonden). 
The work carried out in collaboration with co-workers at Statens Serum Institute (SSI, 
Copenhagen), DNA Diagnostic (Aarhus), Qiagen Aarhus (CLC bio) and DTU CBS. The DNA 
extraction and part of the sequencing were performed at DTU. Pad Lock probe protocols were 
performed at DNA-Diagnostic location in Aarhus. Bioinformatics performed at DTU and QIAGEN 
Aarhus (CLS bio).                     
I would like to thank Dorthe Lau Baggelsen and Iben Bang-Berthelsen for educations possibilities 
and support during my project completion. Likewise, I would like to thank Jeffrey Hoorfar for 
helpful supervision.

==PAGE:3==
Abstract 
Problem definition: As food safety has become an increasing concern for consumers, there is a 
growing need for fast, cost effective and sensitive methods for specific detection and identification 
of low-abundance zoonotic microorganisms. This study is part of an ongoing project, which is 
based on detecting and characterizing pathogenic bacteria, exemplified by Campylobacter, by direct 
metagenomic sequencing of animal and human faecal samples.  
Methods and Results: To produce DNA of sufficient quantity and quality for metagenomic 
sequencing from spiking with Campylobacter jejuni in two levels, fresh and frozen faces samples, 
sample preparation and DNA extraction procedures were optimised and adjusted. Total DNA 
outputs from samples were measured by Qubit 2.0 fluorimeter and confirmed by real-time PCR. 
Five samples with the highest yield of total DNA were chosen for further target enrichment by 
padlock probe technologies. As a target enrichment technology, padlock probes utilize single-
stranded oligonucleotide probes to capture candidate genomic regions from a DNA sample before 
sequencing. Next Generation Sequencing was performed in Illumina MiSeq, and raw Illumina 
readings were paired to generate end bioinformatics data .The sequencing reads were cleaned, 
trimmed and analysed by MGmapper (Center for Genomic Epidemiology, DTU ) and CLC 
Microbial Genomics, which included tools for microbial community analysis as well as tools for 
epidemiological typing of microbial isolates. 
The results of this study are showing significant, almost total degradation of Campylobacter jejuni 
from long period storage at -20°C (up to one year) samples. Degradation was confirmed by Real-
time PCR and conventional cultivation on agar plates. QIAamp DNA Stool Mini Kit (Qiagen) is a 
very good performing protocol regarding total DNA yield from the inoculated chicken faecal 
samples. Padlock concept generally has problems with the existing padlock probe construct, which 
gave negative results from the sample with highest inoculation level and control. False negative 
results at MiSeq'en could be explained by the fact that there were no Illumina (MiSeq) insert for the 
machine to detect the padlock probe. The bioinformatics part of study confirms that both platforms, 
MGmapper (Center for Genomic Epidemiology, DTU) and CLC Microbial Genomics could be used 
for future studies  for microbial community analysis as well as tools for epidemiological typing of 
microbial isolates, but bioinformatics analysis methods are not optimized for samples with very low 
concentrations of bacteria (false negative results).  
Conclusions and future direction  
In order to continue research it would be necessary to find new, or optimized existing DNA 
extraction procedures DNA extraction procedures and construct some new Pad Lock with the 
specific Illumina adapters. MGmapper (Center for Genomic epidemiology, DTU) and CLC 
Microbial Genomics could be used for future studies, but need to include the tools for low-
abundance bacterial strains to avoid false negative results. It would also be interesting to investigate 
 
3 
==PAGE:4==
correlation between the amount of amplifiable DNA, exemplified by Campylobacter, in the 
metagenomic samples, and the outcome of a diagnostic metagenomics analysis for determining the 
lowest concentration of bacteria in faecal samples, that do not give false negative results with the 
current condition of the bioinformatics tools.

1 Introduction 
At the present moment, many diagnostic laboratories are changing their diagnostic setup from 
culturing to molecular analyses on the primary material. The lack of culturing means that bacterial 
isolates are not obtained for additional downstream analyses like virulence-gene profiling, antibiotic 
resistance and typing-analyses for surveillance. The number of clinical isolates is therefore 
decreasing, leaving the current diagnostic and surveillance of foodborne pathogens in crisis. Hence, 
there is an urgent need for a culture-independent typing system. The current project takes advantage 
of recent developments in Next Generation Sequencing (NGS) technologies, bioinformatics and 
DNA extraction by producing an integrated solution that meets the requirement for end-users at 
reference and diagnostic laboratories regarding microbial typing based on primary material. 
The vision is to build an efficient and easy-to-use solution for diagnostics of pathogenic bacteria, 
especially low-abundance bacterial strains, exemplified by Campylobacter.  The idea is to replace 
the current multi-step and culture based detection system of pathogenic gut bacteria with a one-step 
metagenomic approach, where DNA purification is performed directly from complex samples and 
sequences (Figure 1)[1] 
        
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The optimized DNA extraction kits should give us high quality and concentration total DNA,  
wherein the concentration of our DNA could be measured by the Real-Time PCR. Once high 
quality DNA is obtained, metagenomics sequencing of many different microbes en mass will be 
possible, followed by “teasing out” individual genomes or genes to identify those known to be of 
clinical importance. The cost of genome sequencing has been in a steep fall, and the price is 
expected to decrease further in the coming years. Within the NGS community, there is an on-going 
refinement of existing technologies to increase the average length of the DNA strands that can be 
‘read’. This will be useful, as longer reads might be needed to distinguish between closely related 
microorganisms. The scientific idea is to combine an efficient, non-complex and low-cost extraction 
 
7 
==PAGE:8==
kit with the most optimal NGS platform, and afterwards import the generated DNA sequence data 
into the software developed specifically for workflow enabled metagenomic analysis[1]. 
 
2. Literature review 
 
2.1 Introduction to Campylobacter  
2.1.1 Biology 
The genus of Campylobacter belongs to the family Campylobacteraceae, which is comprised of 18 
species and 6 subspecies [2]. There are gram-negative and non-spore-forming bacteria. 
Campylobacter jejuni is the major food-borne pathogen, and therefore this species is the focus in 
this project. All species grow at 37°C, and Campylobacter jejuni have optimal growth temperature 
at 42-45°C. Campylobacter do not grow below 28°C, but have been observed to survive in chilled 
and frozen meat [3]. Campylobacter are sensitive to drying [4]. Under certain unfavourable 
conditions Campylobacter can transform into a “viable-non culturable” state. In this state, they are 
not able to be detected by methods of cultivation, but could however still be infectious [5]. 
2.1.2 Epidemiology 
Campylobacter infections are estimated to be the leading cause of human bacterial gastrointestinal 
disease in many nations. In Denmark 2007, 3868 cases of campylobacteriosis were reported, which 
was the highest number observed in five years [6]. In the same year, 200.507 confirmed cases were 
reported in the European Union) [7]. 
Human Campylobacter infections follow a distinct seasonal pattern. In Denmark, a peak in reported 
incidences is seen in the summer from June to September [6]. Primarily the thermotolerant species 
Campylobacter jejuni and to a lesser extent C. coli are known as the major cause of diarrhoeal 
illness. In European fresh broiler meat, these Campylobacter specie (species?) accounted for 46.2% 
and 17.6% in 2007, respectively [7].   
Campylobacter are widespread in nature, but are mostly associated with the alimentary tract of wild 
and food animals, e.g. poultry, cattle, pigs, goats and pets, and water sources [7]. Other identified 
risk factors are: unpasteurized milk, drinking water, travelling, contact with young domestic 
animals and barbecues. The presence of Campylobacter in meat is usually caused by contamination 
of intestinal content during slaughter and evisceration [8]. The basic routes of transmission of 
Campylobacter are illustrated in Figure 2. 
 
 
8 
==PAGE:9==
 
Figure 2. The sources and basic transmission routes of Campylobacter in the food chain. Campylobacter can infect 
humans from contaminated poultry, drinking water or unpasteurized milk. Adapted from [9].   
2.1.3 Pathogenesis 
Clinical expression of infection 
Human campylobacteriosis is characterized by inflammation, abdominal pain, fever and diarrhoea. 
The infectious dose in healthy humans is as low as 500 bacteria [10], and acute, sometimes bloody, 
watery diarrhoea can be developed in 2-5 days. Although the disease is typically resolved in one 
week, symptoms can last for up to 2 weeks [9].Globally, the disease appears in two different 
manifestations. In developed countries, both adults and children can get infected. In developing 
countries, infection is most frequent among infants and young children. It is likely that this acts as a 
natural vaccine, that prevents them getting infected as adults [9]. 
 
Virulence 
The mechanisms that lead to infection of Campylobacter are yet not fully understood. However, 
there are several properties of this organism that are known to influence host cell invasion and 
pathogenesis. Upon infection, Campylobacter jejuni invades the intestinal epithelial layer in 
humans [11]. 
Flagellin contribute to the bacteria's motility which allows it to travel throughout the host 
[12].Campylobacter jejuni also contains lipopolysaccharides (LPS) in their outer membrane. This 
LPS plays a role in adherence as well as evading the immune system. The bacteria have the ability 
to shift its LPS antigen composition that makes it harder for the immune system to detect the 
pathogen. An important factor in contracting Guillians Barre syndrome from this pathogen is the 
sialic acid that is contained in its core oligosaccharide. This compound can resemble gangliosides 
that can cause this neurological disease. 
Adhesion is a necessary part of virulence for this bacteria because it allows Campylobacter jejuni to 
stay on the host cell long enough to cross into it. Adhesion is possible through various proteins, 
 
9 
==PAGE:10==
flagella, and lipopolysaccharide. The bacteria’s core toxicity pathway is cytolethal distending toxin. 
This toxin stops the cell’s growth cycle in G2 and the cell eventually dies. The cell death that 
accompanies this toxin is a reason for blood in the host’s diarrhea. This toxin is also thought to 
cause immunosuppression. There are three portions of this toxin, CdtA, CdtB, and CdtC. Little is 
known about the exact mechanism the three portions have for carrying out the disease however it is 
known that the CdtB nuclease can disrupt the DNA in the cell [66].  
2.2 Short review of sample preparation, molecular and sequencing methods in 
microbiological food safety 
 
This section provides a presentation and evaluation of the methods, which have been widely used 
for microbiological food safety with focus on detection of Campylobacter. Pre-processing methods 
will be discussed first, followed by phenotypic and genotypic methods. 
2.2.1 Sample preparation 
The aim of sample preparation  in this study is to convert a complex biological samples with its 
target nucleic acids/cells into samples suitable to molecular and sequencing methods. Several 
different sample preparation strategies can be used: optimization of the sampling method, 
optimization of the sample preparation method, and combinations of the different strategies. 
 
Sampling, sample treatment, enrichment 
One of the biggest challenges in diagnostic microbiology is a sampling and sample treatment. 
However, little attention has been paid to the effects of sampling procedure and choice of 
methodology on the output resulting from such studies. There are several practical challenges  
associated with the collection of samples. Ideally, samples should be delivered fast and processed 
directly after delivery. For obvious reasons, however, this normally cannot be achieved, and it is 
thus usually necessary for microbiologists to base their analyses on chilled or frozen samples that 
have been exposed to oxygen. Some reports indicate that for example freezing has a minor 
influence on the composition of extracted bacterial DNA from faeces [13, 14]. Additionally, the 
concentration of the target is sometimes very low, so large samples need to be taken for subsequent 
concentration of target and enrichment step will be necessary. The sampling method should also 
minimize uptake of inhibitory substances [15]. 
A summary of the different sample preparation categories is presented in Table 1. In general, all 
methods are time consuming. DNA extraction methods provide templates of high quality, but the 
methods are usually complex. Physical methods normally do not affect the specificity of the 
molecular protocols as the immunological and physiological methods might. Because of the 
inhibitory components present in the enrichment broth, the simplest method is to take the sample 
 
10 
==PAGE:11==
directly from the enrichment broth and dilute the sample. However, complex matrices present in the 
culture medium may have a detrimental effect on molecular and sequencing method performance 
[16]. 
 
Table1 .Sample Preparation Methods Used for Different Types of Samples. Adapted and modified from [16]. 
 
 
The various factors that need to be considered which are associated with sampling and testing, are 
referred to “Microbiological Criteria” (Commision regulation (EC)No2073/2005). This  include 
sampling strategies, permitted level of sample, and if any, the number of sample units, that can 
exceed the permitted microbe levels and, finally, descriptions of action plan if the permitted level is 
exceeded or pathogens are [17]. 
The enrichment steps can be divided into several groups, dependent on the detection and analysis 
methods. The classical microbiological enrichment is used to increase the small number of a desired 
organism to detectable levels. Enrichment cultures are used because these targeted bacteria may be 
easy to miss on other cultures where they would not grow in populations large enough to be 
detectable. Enrichment step is time consuming and it is often followed by bacteria selection step. 
For increasing the analytical success rate and the robustness of the applied molecular assays, the 
amount of amplifiable DNA can be maximized by optimisation of chemical composition of PCR. 
NGS technology has not yet reached a point at which routine sequencing of large numbers of whole 
eukaryotic genomes is feasible,  so it is often necessary to select genomic regions of interest and to 
enrich these regions before sequencing. There are several enrichment approaches, each with unique 
advantages and disadvantages. Therefore, target-enrichment strategies for next-generation 
sequencing are dealing with compromise between sensitivity, specificity, and uniformity for 
particular projects. 
2.2.2 Molecular methods  
Molecular techniques methodology 
 
11 
==PAGE:12==
Several molecular methods for characterization of microorganisms have been developed, and there 
are often a wide range of phenotypic and genotypic techniques to choose from. The suitability of 
each technique depends on many criteria, with regard to both convenience and performance. 
Criteria of convenience include time to return results, available equipment and trained personnel, 
since some methods are expensive or require personnel med proper training [18]. The important 
performance parameters to consider when assessing the value of a typing method include stability, 
discriminatory power, typeability and reproducibility. These are described in the following sections. 
 
Stability 
A good molecular typing method should result in a fingerprint of a given strain that remains stable 
during the whole study period, both after the primary isolation and during laboratory storage and 
subculture.  Variation in the tested characteristics can contribute to confusion and wrong results can 
be obtained.  
There are several ways to test the stability of typing methods, and the stability should ideally be 
tested each time a typing method is employed for typing a new set of strains. One way to test the 
stability could be to perform multiple subcultivations of the same isolate, stored over different 
periods and under different conditions. If the fingerprint remains the same, the method can be 
considered stable. It is also acceptable to consider a marker to be stable, if multiple isolates of an 
epidemic strain obtained from e. g. different patients at different moments are indistinguishable by 
typing based on that particular marker [18]. 
 
Discriminatory power 
The discriminatory power of a typing method can be expressed mathematically as a probability by 
using Simpson’s Index of Diversity. This index describes the ability of the method to distinguish 
between two unrelated strains, which have been sampled randomly from the population of a given 
species [19]. A modification of Simpson’s Index of Diversity was developed by Hunter and Gaston 
[19]. This provided a single numerical index of discriminatory capacity, which has been widely 
used in assessing the discriminatory power of typing methods. Hunter’s index of diversity is given 
by the following equation: 
 
∑

Where N is the total number of strains in the sample population, S is the total number of types 
described, and nj is the number of strains belonging to the jth type. The ideal typing method should 
have an index of 1.00, but this is rarely obtained in practice. It is though generally accepted by most 
 
12 
==PAGE:13==
professionals in the field that an index of an ideal typing method should be at least in the order of 
0.95 [18]. 
 
Typeability 
The typeability of a method describes how many strains of a given dataset that can be assigned a 
certain type. For most methods, it is common that with certain isolates a clear result cannot be 
obtained. The typeability is expressed as a percentage of typeable isolates over the total number of 
typed isolates [18, 19, 20]. 
 
Reproducibility 
The reproducibility of a typing method refers to the ability of obtaining the exact same fingerprint 
of an isolate when it is tested on independent occasions, separated in time and/or place [21]. There 
are many factors that can influence the reproducibility of a typing method. These include factors 
from the data generation in general (e.g. preparation of materials, different batches of reagents, 
reagent variation as a result of local preparation and different types of equipment) and the data 
interpretation (e.g. wrong observations and record of the results and analysis and interpretation of 
results).  In order to ensure reproducibility, both internal and external, standardized protocols and 
training of personnel are required [18, 20]. 
 
2.2.3 Phenotypic methods  
 
"Phenotype" is an organism's actual observed properties, such as morphology, development, 
or behaviour. Phenotypic typing methods are based on detection or characterization of products of 
gene expression. This can be measured qualitatively as the ability of an isolate to grow or behave in 
the presence of specific substances (e.g. antibiotics, toxins or bacteriophages) or quantitatively as 
the expression of specific molecules (e.g. antigens or enzymes). Also biochemical profiles can be 
assessed by measurements of different metabolic reactions, which are known as biotyping [18].  
The olds phenotypic methods is a “Gram”-method. The Gram stain, developed in 1884 by Hans 
Christian Gram, characterizes bacteria based on the structural characteristics of their cell walls. The 
thick layers of peptidoglycan in the "Gram-positive" cell wall stain purple, while the thin "Gram-
negative" cell wall appears pink. By combining morphology and Gram-staining, most bacteria can 
be classified as belonging to one of four groups (Gram-positive cocci, Gram-positive bacilli, Gram-
negative cocci, and Gram-negative bacilli). Some organisms are best identified by stains other than 
the Gram stain, particularly mycobacteria or Nocardia, which show acid-fastness on Ziehl–Neelsen 
or similar stains. Other organisms may need to be identified by their growth in special media, or by 
other techniques, such as serology [22]. 
 
13 
==PAGE:14==
There are several phenotypic techniques such “old fashion” biochemical tests, phage typing, 
antimicrobial resistance monitoring, serotyping, multilocus enzyme electrophoresis (MLEE), 
protein profiling – SDS PAGE, immunoblotting, techniques based on nutritional requirement e.g. 
auxotyping, biotyping, bacteriocin typing. 
Serotyping is based on antigenetic determinants expressed on the cell surface. This method is still 
widely used for Salmonella, Shigella, Neiseria, E. coli, V cholerae. The technique is based on slide/ 
tube agglutination. It is a robust method, but requires extensive stock of absorbed/monoclonal sera 
(e.g. >2200 antisera required for definitive Salmonella typing). 
Phage typing is based on ability of viruses infecting and destroying bacterial cells – Bacteriophage. 
The resistance or susceptibility of strains to viruses is used for differentiation and classification of 
bacteria. Phage typing that is imitated by technically demanding, time consuming, and typeability is 
an issue.  
Antibiotic susceptibility testing is based on susceptibility of bacterial isolates to a panel of 
antimicrobial agents. This test is routinely performed on clinical isolates in many countries and an 
reasonable preliminary indicator to initiate epidemiological action. But antibiotic resistance under 
extraordinary selective pressure and multiple mechanisms affect the strains to become abruptly 
resistant. Phenotypic characteristics can vary in different conditions, antibiotic resistance can be 
expressed under antibiotic pressure, and generally methods are not very discriminatory. 
Multilocus enzyme electrophoresis method (MLEE) characterizes the cellular proteins by 
electrophoretically separating them in a gel matrix, but interpretation of results is very complex and 
time consuming.  
Phenotypic methods has some advantages and limitations regarding typeability, reproducibility and 
discrimination (Table 2). 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Table 2.Phenotypic typic system characretistics. Adapted from [67]. 
 
14 
==PAGE:15==
 
 
When comparing phenotyping with genotyping for typing of Campylobacter, it is clear that 
genotyping has several advantages. It is difficult to characterize an organism with high genetic 
diversity such as C. jejuni by phenotyping, since a phenotype can have several genotypes [23]. The 
discrimination of a phenotyping test is therefore generally poor compared to a genotyping test. 
Phenotypes are also much more susceptible to changes in environmental conditions than genotypes, 
and do therefore require adequate quality control and standardisation of both reagents and method. 
They are also very subjective and require a great deal of skill and experience [24]. These 
disadvantages combined with the fact that sequencing is getting cheaper, result in less frequent use 
of these methods. 
Serotyping has been one of the most commonly used phenotyping methods employed for 
Campylobacter since the 1980s. For typing of Campylobacter species, the assay is performed by the 
use of the thermostable antigen (Penner scheme) or the heat-labile antigen (Lior scheme) [25].  
Serotyping of C. jejuni has been shown to be useful in distinguishing between outbreak and non-
outbreak strains [26].However, several disadvantages have been associated with this method, such 
as low discriminatory power and low typeability.  
Multilocus enzyme electrophoresis (MLEE) has also been employed for typing of Campylobacter. 
This method relies on the differentiation of protein size and charge of 9-11 housekeeping enzymes. 
An electrophoretic type is determined for each isolate based on the profile of the analysed enzymes. 
Though the method is no longer widely used, it is important to mention since it provided the basis 
 
15 
==PAGE:16==
for Multilocus sequence typing (MLST), which today is a gold standard for typing of 
Campylobacter [18, 22].  
 
Though genotyping methods are mainly used today, a new generation of modern phenotyping 
approaches based on physical techniques are increasingly applied and developed. These methods 
provide molecular fingerprints of entire organisms, based on different spectra measurements. 
Examples of techniques are Matrix assisted laser desorption ionization time of flight Mass 
Spectrometry (MALDI-TOF-MS), Infrared or Raman spectroscopy and Fourier transform infrared 
spectroscopy (FTIR) [18]. 
 
 2.2.4 Genotypic methods 
 
Genotyping is the process of determining differences in the genotype by examining the 
individual's DNA sequence using different assays and comparing it to another individual's sequence 
or a reference sequence. Traditionally genotyping is the use of DNA sequences to define biological 
populations by use of molecular tools. Genotypic typing methods characterize microorganisms 
according to variations at the genetic level, in terms of composition of nucleotide bases, gene order 
and gene functions. Current methods of genotyping include  pulsed field gel electrophoresis (PFGE) 
and restriction fragment length polymorphism identification (RFLPI) of genomic DNA, random 
amplified polymorphic 
detection(RAPD) 
of 
genomic 
DNA, amplified fragment length 
polymorphism detection 
(AFLPD), polymerase 
chain 
reaction (PCR),  
allele 
specific 
oligonucleotide (ASO) probes,  hybridization to DNA microarrays or beads, and DNA sequencing. 
Genotyping is important in research of genes and gene variants associated with disease. Due to 
current technological limitations, almost all genotyping is partial. Genotyping in this context may 
help in controlling the spreading of pathogens, by tracing the origin of outbreaks.  Most of 
genotyping methods have proper typeability, reproducibility and discrimination (Table 3)[68].  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
16 
==PAGE:17==
 
Table 3.  Genotypic typing system characteristic. Adapted from [67]. 
 
 
Many genotypic typing methodologies has been applied for typing of Campylobacter, however only 
some of the methods will be presented here. The genotypic methods can be divided into two general 
approaches: restriction and/or amplification based methods and sequencing based methods [27].  
Restriction site and/or amplification based methods 
Most genotypic schemes rely on the nucleotide differences between strains that alter the genomic 
restriction fragment pattern [28]. Restriction based typing methods, which have been widely used 
for typing of Campylobacter include two groups of methods, those which are based solely on 
restriction and those which also include steps of amplification.  
The methods based on restriction only are pulsed field gel electrophoresis (PFGE), restriction 
fragment length polymorphism (RFLP) analysis and ribotyping. The amplification and restriction 
based typing methods include amplified fragment length polymorphism (AFLP) and randomly 
amplified polymorphic DNA (RAPD) [27].  
 
Typing by PFGE and RFLP is based on digestion of genomic DNA by restriction endonucleases 
cutting at different frequencies, which results in a number of large DNA fragments of various sizes. 
The DNA fragments are subsequently separated by gel electrophoresis. The gel images constitute a 
restriction profile of the isolates, which are used as basis for typing. Gel images can be examined 
manually and also by computerised processing [27].  
 
17 
==PAGE:18==
PFGE is one of the most important typing methods since it is the most widely used whole genomic 
approaches and is considered as a gold standard for typing of Campylobacter [29]. It has been 
applied for typing of Campylobacter in numerous studies and was found to be a highly 
discriminatory and reproducible method, with very high typeability [30]. Drawbacks of PFGE are 
that it is time consuming, expensive, and needs experienced personnel [27]. However, it is a huge 
advance that the obtained data can be shared between laboratories by international networks 
(PulseNet), since this enables rapid recognition of multinational outbreaks [31]. 
Ribotyping is based on the location and number of ribosomal RNA, applying the same principle as 
for RFLP. Subsequently restriction and separation of DNA fragments is carried out and the 
fragments are transferred to a membrane and Southern blot hybridisation with a probe specific for 
rRNA genes is performed. The discriminatory power of ribotyping is usually low [32].  
AFLP is a whole genome typing method where chromosomal DNA is digested with frequent 
cutting restriction enzymes. The restriction fragments are then ligated to specific adaptors, which 
are containing sequences with the complementary sequence to the restriction enzyme sites. PCR 
primers, of which one is labelled for detection, bind to the adaptors and amplify the restriction 
fragments. Only the fragments flanked by both restriction sites are amplified. Fragment separation 
is performed using a DNA sequencing instrument [33].AFLP has been shown to have a 
discriminatory power similar to PFGE and can be used for a large number of bacteria [30]. The  
technique is though quite complex and expensive, since an automated DNA sequencer and software 
for analysis is needed. 
 RAPD is also a whole genome typing method, were short unspecific primers hybridise at random 
chromosomal positions and initiates amplification. The stringency conditions are low, and only if 
primers anneal to both strands in reasonable proximity, and DNA is synthesised between these, a 
PCR product will be produced. The resulting PCR products will vary in size, and a DNA fingerprint 
of the isolate will be formed when subjected to agarose gel electrophoresis [27]. 
All methods which are based on restriction and/or amplification suffer from the same general 
drawbacks. Comparison of data between laboratories is difficult, since standardized sets of 
restriction enzymes are not available for many protocols. Differences in DNA quality, PCR 
conditions, digestion conditions and electrophoretic conditions do also lead to differences in profile 
obtained at different laboratories. In addition, some strains are not typeable by these methods since 
they express a particular methylation pattern, which prohibits restriction by some nucleases [28]. 
 
Sequencing based methods 
Sequencing based typing methods applied for Campylobacter include flaA SVR sequencing, 
Multilocus Sequence Typing (MLST), and single nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) genotyping. 
 
18 
==PAGE:19==
flaA SVR sequencing is based on amplification and sequencing of a specific short variable region 
(SVR) of the flagellin gene flaA [34]. flaA SVR sequencing is also of main focus in this report and 
is described in details in section 2.5.2.  
MLST is based on amplification and sequencing of seven housekeeping genes. The nucleotide 
sequences of these genes provide profiles which are referred to as sequence types [35]. The MLST 
approach is a highly reproducible, and the method is considered as a gold standard for typing of 
campylobacters. It is considered to be highly accurate, and comparable between laboratories, due to 
the easy interpretation of results via the internet [35].  
SNP genotyping characterizes isolates based on the identification of single nucleotides at predefined 
positions, which are known to be variable within the population. The SNP can be detected by 
Sanger sequencing, pyrosequencing, mass spectrometry or real time PCR assays [27]. C. jejuni SNP 
genotyping has been developed based on the SNPs at specific positions on the seven housekeeping 
genes, which are also used in MLST [36]. This has been shown to be a rapid and cheap 
complementary alternative to MLST, since it can detect the clonal complexes of C. jejuni. Though 
the resolution is lower than for MLST, it can function as a screening tool to rapidly confirm the 
presence of certain clonal complexes [36]. 
2.2.3 Next Generation Sequencing metods 
 
Milestones in whole genome sequencing 
In bacteriology, the genomic era began in 1995, when the first bacterial genome was sequenced 
using conventional Sanger sequencing1. Back then, sequencing projects required six-figure budgets 
and years of effort. A decade later, in 2005, the advent of the first high-throughput (or ‘next-
generation’) sequencing technologies signalled a significant advance in the ease and cost of 
sequencing , delivering bacterial genome sequences in hours or days rather than months or years. 
High-throughput sequencing now delivers sequence data thousands of times more cheaply than is 
possible with Sanger sequencing. As shown by the impressive increase in genomic data output 
(Figure 3), whole genome sequencing (WGS) has entered all research laboratories, and will soon 
become an integrated tool in diagnostic laboratories. 
 
19 
==PAGE:20==
 
Figure 3. Milestones in whole genome sequencing. The number of complete and draft genomes from the Archaea 
(n = 172), Bacteria (n = 3625) and Eukarya (n = 183) deposited in public databases from 1995 to 2012 is shown, 
as extracted from the GOLD database (www.genomesonline.org/) in December 2012. The advent of high-
throughput sequencing led to a rapid increase in the number of complete genome sequences (green), but the 
short read length, in particular, triggered the final publication of draft genome sequences (orange). EHEC, 
enterohaemorrhagic Escherichia coli. Adapted from [38]. 
 
A variety of approaches 
The availability of a growing abundance of platforms and instruments presents the user with an 
embarrassment of choice. Better still, vigorous competition between manufacturers has resulted in 
sustained technical improvements on almost all platforms. This means that in recent years our 
sequencing capability has been doubling every 6–9 months [37]. 
High-throughput sequencing platforms can be divided into two broad groups depending on the kind 
of template used for the sequencing reactions.  
The earliest, and currently most widely used, platforms depend on the production of libraries of 
clonally amplified templates. These are produced through amplification of immobilized libraries 
made from a single DNA molecule in the initial sample. More recently, we have seen the arrival of 
single-molecule sequencing platforms, which determine the sequence of single molecules without 
amplification. Within these broad categories, there is considerable variation in performance — 
including in throughput, read length and error rate — as well as in factors affecting usability, such 
as cost and run time. 
 
Template amplification technologies 
In general terms, all of the platforms that are currently on the market rely on a three-stage workflow 
of library preparation, template amplification and sequencing (Figure 4).  
 
20 
==PAGE:21==
 
 Figure 4.  High-throughput sequencing platforms. The schematic shows the main high-throughput sequencing 
platforms available to microbiologists today, and the associated sample preparation and template amplification 
procedures. Adapted  from [39]. 
 
Library preparation begins with the extraction and purification of genomic DNA.  Depending on the 
protocol, the amount of DNA required can vary from a few nanograms to tens of micrograms, 
meaning that success in this step depends on the ability to grow sufficient biomass.  
Therefore, before using expensive reagents for library preparation and sequencing, it is advisable to 
confirm, by fluorometry, that DNA of sufficient quantity and quality has been obtained [38]. 
 For shotgun sequencing, an initial fragmentation step is required to generate random, overlapping 
DNA fragments. Depending on the platform and application, these fragments can range from 150 
bp to 800 bp in length; size selection either involves harvesting from agarose gels or exploits 
 
21 
==PAGE:22==
paramagnetic-bead-based technology. Two types of fragmentation are widely used: mechanical and 
enzymatic.  Early protocols relied on mechanical methods such as nebulization or ultrasonication 
More recently, enzymatic methods have provided an alternative approach to producing random 
fragments of the desired length. These require less input DNA and offer easier, faster sample 
processing. Fragmentase (from New England Biolabs) is a mixture of a nuclease, which randomly 
nicks double-stranded DNA, and a T7 endonuclease, which cleaves the DNA. Together, these 
enzymes generate random double-strand DNA breaks in a time-dependent manner, allowing the 
user to tailor protocols in order to obtain products of the required length. Adaptors can then be 
ligated to these fragments in the usual way. Tagmentation4 is a promising transposase-based 
approach that, in a single step, fragments DNA and incorporates sequence tags, which then take the 
place of adaptors. Currently, the only available implementation of tagmentation is within the 
Nextera system, which is only available for the Illumina platform [38].Several companies have 
produced automated liquid-handling machines that greatly reduce the hands-on time required for 
fragmentation approaches but significantly increase costs [38]. 
In addition to supporting fragment-based sequencing, all template amplification platforms support 
mate pair sequencing, in which the ends of DNA fragments of a certain size (typical sizes are 3 kb, 
6 kb, 8 kb or 20 kb) are joined together to form circular molecules. These molecules are then frag-
mented a second time. Fragments flanking the joins are then selected and end adaptors added. 
Sequencing through the joins provides valuable information about the location of sequences 
dispersed across the genome, facilitating assembly [38]. 
Paired-end sequencing has similarities to mate pair sequencing, but DNA fragments are sequenced 
from each end without the need for additional library preparation steps. The Illumina platform has 
direct support for paired-end sequencing. Short fragments that are less than the read length from the 
forward and reverse ends (for example, 180 bp fragments combined with 2 × 100 base sequencing) 
permits overlapping pseudo long reads to be generated. Alternatively, fragments of up to ~800 bp 
can be used. Longer fragments may result in a loss of amplification efficiency. The Ion Personal 
Genome Machine (PGM) (using the Ion Torrent platform, from Life Technologies) also has a 
bidirectional sequencing protocol that requires the removal of the chip after the initial run, a 
digestion step and a second sequencing run using a different sequencing primer.All platforms can 
handle PCR products, allowing adaptor sequences to be incorporated into the 5ʹ ends of primers 
[38]. 
In preparation for amplification, template molecules are immobilized on a solid surface, which is a 
flow cell for sequencing with the Illumina platform and solid beads or ion sphere particles for other 
approaches. Simultaneous solid-phase amplification of millions or billions of spatially separated 
template fragments prepares the way for massively parallel sequencing. For the Illumina platform, 
template amplification is automated and is performed either directly on the instrument (for the 
MiSeq, and the HiSeq 2500 sequencer in rapid-run mode) or using the cBot, a separate instrument 
 
22 
==PAGE:23==
that is dedicated to this task (used in conjunction with the Genome Analyzer IIx and the HiSeq 2000 
machine). Clusters are generated by bridge amplification on the surface of the flow cell. For 
platforms that use bead-based immobilization (the SOLiD (from Life Technologies), 454 and Ion 
Torrent platforms), amplified template sequence libraries are prepared off-instrument, relying on an 
emulsion PCR, in which the beads are enclosed in aqueous-phase microreactors and are kept 
separated from each other in a water-in-oil emulsion [38]. 
 
Sequencing chemistry 
Although these platforms rely on a sequencing-by-synthesis design, they differ in the details of the 
sequencing chemistry and the approach used to read the sequence. The Illumina sequencing 
platform depends on Solexa chemistry5, which includes reversible termination of sequencing 
products. In each sequencing cycle, a mixture of fluorescently labelled ‘reversible terminator’ 
nucleotides with protected 3ʹ-OH groups (and a different emission wavelength for each nucleotide) 
is perfused across the flow cell. Wherever a complementary nucleotide is present on the template 
strand, the terminator is incorporated and imaged, and then the signal is quenched and the 
terminator nucleotide is chemically deprotected at the 3ʹ-OH group[40]. 
The 454 and Ion Torrent sequencing platforms avoid the use of terminators. Instead, in each cycle a 
single kind of dNTP is flowed across the template. When there is base complementarity between the 
dNTP and the next available position in the template, the DNA polymerase incorporates the base 
onto the extending strand, liberating pyrophosphate and hydrogen ions. When there is no 
complementarity, DNA synthesis is halted temporarily; each type of dNTP is flowed across the 
template in turn according to the dispensing cycle, and DNA synthesis is thus re-initiated when the 
next complementary dNTP is added. The 454 platform exploits a pyrosequencing approach6,7 
whereby the presence of pyrophosphate is signalled by visible light as the result of an enzyme 
cascade. The order and intensity of the light peaks are recorded as ‘flowgrams’. The Ion Torrent 
platform relies on a modified silicon chip to detect hydrogen ions that are released during base 
incorporation; the resulting lack of reliance on imaging makes this platform the first ‘post-light’ 
sequencing instrument[40].  
The SOLiD platform9 and the platform from Complete Genomics10 depend on sequencing by 
ligation. In this approach, fluorescent probes undergo iterative steps of hybridization and ligation to 
complementary positions in the template strand at the 5ʹ end of the extending strand, followed by 
fluorescence imaging to identify the legated probe [38]. 
 
Choosing a platform 
The sequencing market giving the user a choice between different platforms depended of user needs 
(Table 4). 
 
 
 
23 
==PAGE:24==
Table 4. Comparison of 2nd gen. sequencing platforms and methods. Adapted from [40]. 
 
 
 
Single-molecule sequencing 
The HeliScope Single-Molecule Sequencer (from Helicos BioSciences) was the first platform for 
single-molecule sequencing to hit the market place in 2009 [38]. This technology applies one-colour 
reversible-terminator sequencing to unamplified single-molecule templates. However, this platform 
has been hampered by its high price and poor instrument sales and, following the delisting of the 
company from the stock market, there are significant doubts over the future of the platform.  
More recently, Pacific Biosciences has delivered ‘real-time sequencing’, in which dye-labelled 
nucleotides are continuously incorporated into a growing DNA strand by strand-displacing 
φ29-derived DNA polymerase [39]. Each DNA polymerase molecule is tethered within a zero-
mode waveguide detector, which allows continuous imaging of the labelled nucleotides as they 
enter the strand [41]. 
 
Metagenomics 
Metagenomics involves collecting samples from an environment and then extracting and studying 
the genetic material from the microorganisms present in these samples [42]. This approach is 
transforming microbiology, ecology, medicine, and other research areas investigating various 
microbiomes, allowing us to analyze for the first time microbial species, including those not 
culturable, at a level of detail not previously possible [43]. Metagenomics sequence reads can be 
 
24 
==PAGE:25==
taxonomically classified to identify the microbes, or functionally classified (gene functions, 
metabolic pathways, etc.) to identify the functional potential of the community. There exist two 
general approaches for characterizing the taxonomic content of environmental samples: (1) 
sequencing of PCR amplicons corresponding to phylogenetic marker genes (e.g. 16S rRNA; 
“amplicon analysis”); (2) shotgun sequencing whereby all genomic DNA in the community is 
sequenced [44]. 
The first metagenomic studies conducted using high-throughput sequencing used massively 
parallel 454 pyrosequencing. Other technologies commonly applied to environmental sampling are 
the Ion Torrent Personal Genome Machine, the Illumina MiSeq or HiSeq and the Applied 
Biosystems SOLiD system.These techniques for sequencing DNA generate shorter fragments 
than Sanger sequencing; Ion Torrent PGM System and 454 pyrosequencing typically produces 
~400 bp reads, Illumina MiSeq produces 400-700bp reads (depending on whether paired end 
options are used), and SOLiD produce 25-75 bp reads. 
 Shotgun metagenomics randomly shears DNA, sequences many short sequences, 
and reconstructs them into a consensus sequence. Shotgun metagenomics provides information both 
about which organisms are present and what metabolic processes are possible in the 
community. Because the collection of DNA from an environment is largely uncontrolled, the most 
abundant organisms in an environmental sample are most highly represented in the resulting 
sequence data. To achieve the high coverage needed to fully resolve the genomes of under-
represented community members, large samples, often prohibitively so, are needed. On the other 
hand, the random nature of shotgun sequencing ensures that many of these organisms, which would 
otherwise go unnoticed using traditional culturing techniques, will be represented by at least some 
small sequence segments [69].  
 
Bioinformatics 
Historically, the term bioinformatics did not mean what it means today. Paulien Hogeweg and Ben 
Hesper coined it in 1970 to refer to the study of information processes in biotic systems. Today  
bioinformatics is an interdisciplinary field that develops methods and software tools for 
understanding biological data.  
Databases are essential for bioinformatics research and applications. There ere a huge numbers of 
available databases covering almost everything from DNA and protein sequences, molecular 
structures, to phenotypes and biodiversity. Databases generally fall into one of three types. Some 
contain data resulting directly from empirical methods such as gene knockouts. Others consist of 
predicted data, and most contain data from both sources. There are meta-databases that incorporate 
data compiled from multiple other databases. Some others are specialized, such as those specific to 
an organism. These databases vary in their format, way of accession and whether they are public or 
not. 
 
25 
==PAGE:26==
Software tools for bioinformatics range from simple command-line tools, to more complex 
graphical programs and standalone web-services available from various bioinformatics 
companies or public institutions. There are present different open-source bioinformatics software, 
web-services and bioinformatics workflow management systems [70]. 
 
Pipeline  
Recent advances in whole genome sequencing (WGS) have made the technology available for 
routine use in microbiological laboratories. However, a major obstacle for using this technology is 
the availability of simple and automatic bioinfomatics tools. Based on previously published and 
already available web-based tools CBS developed a single pipeline for batch uploading WGS data 
from multiple bacterial isolates. The pipeline will automatically identify the bacterial species, 
assemble the genomes and, if applicable, identify MLST type, plasmids, virulence genes and 
antimicrobial resistance genes. A short printable report for each isolates will be provided and an 
interactive list of all sequences including those submitted previously is made available for the user. 
The pipeline was tested using WGS data from 239 salmonella isolates divided into 13 batch 
submissions. The platform is freely available at: https://cge.cbs.dtu.dk/services/CGEpipeline-1.0 
and it is the intention that it will continue to be expanded with new features, when these are 
available [71].  
 
2.3 Theory behind applied methods  
2.3.1 Isolation of genomic DNA 
 
One from to chosen kits for DNA purification in this study was QIAamp DNA Stool Mini Kit 
(Qiagen, Cat No./ID51504).  The QIAamp DNA Stool Mini Kit provides silica membrane-based 
purification of up to 30 μg genomic, bacterial, viral, and parasite DNA from fresh or frozen human 
stool or other sample types with high concentrations of PCR inhibitors. Up to 220 mg stool can be 
processed routinely, and larger amounts can be processed with additional Buffer ASL. Typical 
yields of 10–30 µg are obtained in 50 minutes, and DNA is eluted in 200 µl.  The purified DNA is 
sized up to 50 kb. DNA of this length denatures completely and has the highest amplification 
efficiency. The combined action of InhibitEX, a adsorption resin, and an optimized buffer leads to 
removal of PCR inhibitors. The convenient QIAamp spin-column procedure provides purification in 
only 50 minutes. Purification of DNA using the QIAamp DNA Stool Mini Kit can be automated on 
the QIAcube [72]. 
Principle 
DNA binds specifically to the QIAamp silica-gel membrane while contaminants pass through. No 
phenol–chloroform extraction is required. PCR inhibitors are removed by the combined action of 
 
26 
==PAGE:27==
InhibitEX, a unique adsorption resin, and an optimized buffer. Rigorous lysis using proteinase K 
ensures high yields of all types of DNA common in stool, including colorectal epithelial cells, 
bacteria, viruses and other gastrointestinal pathogens. 
Highly pure DNA ready for direct use in downstream amplification reactions is purified in about 50 
minutes. QIAamp sample preparation technology is fully licensed[72]. 
Procedure 
The QIAamp DNA Stool Mini Kit simplifies isolation of DNA from stool with a fast spin-column 
procedure. Optimized buffers and enzymes lyse samples, stabilize nucleic acids, and enhance 
selective DNA adsorption to the QIAamp membrane. Alcohol is added and lysates loaded onto the 
QIAamp spin column. PCR inhibitors are removed by the combined action of InhibitEX and an 
optimized buffer. Proteinase K lysis ensures high yields of all types of DNA common in stool. 
Remaining impurities are efficiently removed in two wash steps. Amplification-ready DNA is then 
eluted in low-salt buffer[72].  
The second kit was Genomic DNA isolation (Easy-DNA™ Kit, cat.no. K1800-01, Invitrogen,) 
The Easy-DNA™ Kit is a simple, quick, and inexpensive method for the isolation of DNA from a 
variety of sources. DNA from the sources listed has been successfully isolated and used to produce 
PCR products or for Southern blot experiments, fresh, dried, frozen, or heparinized blood tissue, 
culture cells, both suspended and trypsinized, mammalian tissue , plant leaves ,hair follicles ,mouse 
tails, baculovirus (viral particles). 
 
Principle and Procedure 
Cells are lysed by the addition of Solution A and subsequent incubation at 65°C. Proteins and lipids 
are precipitated and extracted by the addition of Solution B and chloroform. The solution is then 
centrifuged to separate the solution into two phases with a solid interface separating the two phases. 
The DNA is in the upper, clear aqueous phase, the proteins and lipids are in the solid interface, and 
the chloroform forms the lower phase. The DNA is then removed, precipitated with ethanol, and 
resuspended in TE buffer. Purified DNA may be used for PCR or restriction digestion, or may be 
stored at 4°C [73].  
2.3.2 Real-time PCR 
 
Higuchi [45] introduced for the first time a way to detect specific DNA sequences, using the 
polymerase chain reaction (PCR) without opening the reaction tube. The new attempt of [45] leads 
to a revolution in PCR techniques. Not only is it now possible to detect the amplicons without 
opening the tube, it is also possible to observe the multiplying of the DNA in real-time and to obtain 
a quantitative result immediately after the run, without further handling of the samples.  
Principle 
 
27 
==PAGE:28==
The purpose of PCR is to multiply DNA molecules and to make multiple copies of a selected DNA 
sequence in a short time.  The PCR reaction itself is an iterative process, based on a temperature 
cycle using three steps [46]: (1) Denaturation:  High temperature is applied to separate (melt) the 
strands of the double stranded DNA into single stranded DNA (ssDNA). (2)Annealing: The 
temperature is lowered to let primers anneal to the template DNA. (3) Elongation: The temperature 
is set around 72˚C, which is the optimum for the polymerase that extends the primers by 
incorporating the dNTPs.  
To perform a PCR, following components are crucial [46]:(1)Template DNA: DNA from the 
sample that is to be copied. Primers: Two primers are needed, a forward and a reverse primer. 
(2)Primers are short single stranded oligonucleotides that are complimentary to the DNA sequence 
of interest. One primer is located at the beginning and one at the end of the target region. The 
primers define which part of the DNA molecule will be copied or amplified. (3)Thermostable DNA 
polymerase: This is an enzyme that replicates DNA. The DNA polymerase used in PCR needs to be 
resistant to high temperatures (thermostable). (4) Desoxynucleotide triphosphates (dNTP): dNTPs 
are the building blocks used to make DNA copies. dNTPs of all four bases are added – dATP,  
dCTP, dGTP and dTTP/dUTP. (5)Buffer solution: This solution contains Mg2+ and other salts and 
chemicals which help the DNA polymerase to function. 
(6)Fluorescent reporter / Probe: This component is only necessary for real-time PCR. A fluorescent 
reporter binds to the product formed, and reports its presence by fluorescence. These reporters 
might be probe-based, primer-based or DNA binding dyes [47]. 
PCR ware proformed on StepOnePlus™ Real-Time PCR System with  LED-based optical system 
that can record fluorescence from FAM™/SYBR® Green, VIC®/JOE™, NED™/TAMRA™, and 
ROX™ dyes. This cost-effective, 4-color, 96-well system delivers precise, quantitative Real-Time 
PCR results and saves data from all filters in every run without depending on a computer or plate 
setup. Fluorescence measurements were analysed online and StepOnePlus Software v.2.3 (Applised 
Biosystem). 
2.3.3 Pad Lock probes  
 
Targeted next-generation sequencing (NGS) requires  substantial improvements in performance, 
cost and multiplex sample processing to be applied in diagnostic molecular testing . There are now 
several target enrichment strategies with the common goal to capture candidate genomic regions at 
high accuracy and completeness, while lowering the costs at the same time. The most widely used 
methods utilize multiplex PCR amplification [48], hybrid-capture [49], selective target 
circularization [50], and oligonucleotide-selective sequencing. Hybrid-capture methods are quickly 
scalable and have achieved high levels of uniformity in whole-exome sequencing (WES) [51] , 
 
28 
==PAGE:29==
while circularization-based methods can provide increased sensitivity and specificity for variant 
detection in candidate gene. 
A common feature of these methods is their reliance on single-stranded oligonucleotides that serve 
as capturing probes during target enrichment. These probes can be synthesized chemically at high 
quality by column-based synthesis, or at relatively lower quality and cost using programmable 
microarrays [52].The cost-effective synthesis of long (≥150mer) oligonucleotides at high yield and 
quality has remained most difficult [53]. This has been a limitation particularly for the construction 
of Pad Lock probes and the molecular inversion probe (MIP) technology [54], which could benefit 
from probes that are much longer than what can be chemically synthesized. Previously developed  
method for the construction of long Pad Lock probes (LPPs), which are single-stranded DNA 
probes of approximately 320 bases in length  provide high target specificity through dual 
recognition of target sequences [55].LPPs enabled capturing sequences from 98%  exons in 524 
genes using only a single probe [56] . To construct the single-stranded LPPs (ssLPPs) required 
removing one strand from a double-stranded precursor molecule using exonuclease digestion. This 
protocol consisted of a number of laborious steps of enzymatic digestions, de- and re-
phosphorylation (Figure 5)[57]. In some cases, difficulty controlling probe quality and quantity 
during production led to a loss of probes. Now it is possible to syntaside multiplex target capture 
with complementary LPPs (cLPPs), which are double-stranded probes that act as two independent 
single-stranded probes during target capture. cLPPs are easily produced through PCR and 
outperformed ssLPPs and can be built in less than half the time of previous probe construction 
(Figure 5) [57]. 
 
 
Figure 5.  Probe construction, target capture and reciprocal paired-end sequencing. Adapted from[57]. 
 
 
29 
==PAGE:30==
Briefly, each cLPP contains a common linker flanked bypost-capture amplification sites (red and 
green) and two target-specific capturing arms (blue and orange). Probe ends are trimmed (BsaI 
andMlyI) and 5’-phosphorylated to produce functional cLPPs (Figure 5,A)[57] Multiplex probe-
target hybridization followed by gap-filling and ligation triggers probe circularization and target 
capture(Figure 5,B).Capture libraries are multiplex-amplified using hybrid primers that anneal to 
the probes’ amplification sites and add Illumina sequencing adaptors (P5 or P7) and sample-specific 
barcodes. This is done in two separate PCRs during which the adaptors swap positions at the ends 
of templates. Both PCRs are pooled for reciprocal PE sequencing of both DNA strands(Figure 
5,C)[57]. 
2.3.4 NGS 
 
The second next-generation sequencing technology IlluminaSolexa sequencing platform with SBS 
(Sequencing By Synthesis) technology was udes for this study. Advanteges of Illumina MySeq 
were cost effectiveness, minimal hand-on time, massive throughput. Platform can produse 750,000 
Gb per run with reads length 2*100 bases.SBS technology  fluorescently labeled nucleotides is used 
as terminating base which can be removed to leave an unblocked 3' terminus, making chain 
termination a reversible process (Figure 6)[74].The sequencing processing starts with DNA 
fragments ligated with specific adapters and, after denaturation, the sequencing templates are 
immobilized at one end on a flow cell surface which is coated thickly with the adapters and the 
complementary adapters. Each single-strand fragment with one end immobilized on the surface will 
create a “bridge” by hybridizing with its free end to the complementary adapter on the surface of 
the support and following PCR amplification [74]. 
 
Figure 6.  Cyclic reversible terminator sequencing. Adapted from [74].  
 
 
30 
==PAGE:31==
After solid-phase amplification, up to 1,000 identical copies of each single-stranded DNA template 
molecule (called DNA ‘polonies’, similar to cell colonies after polymerase amplification) are 
created on the surface. During each sequencing cycle, a single labeled dNTP is added to the nucleic 
acid chain and after incorporation; the fluorescent dye is imaged by the CCD camera (Figure 6) to 
identify the base and then enzymatically cleaved to allow incorporation of the next nucleotide[74]. 
 
 
Fig 6. Fluorescent dye is imaged by the CCD camera. Adapted from [74]. 
 
Output of sequencing fastq reads were analysed by bioinformatics tools. 
2.3.5 Bioinformatics 
One of two bioinformatical platforms used for this study was CLC Genomics Workbench – a cross-
platform desktop application with a graphical user-interface for analyzing and visualizing next 
generation sequencing data, incorporates cutting-edge technology and algorithms, while also 
supporting and integrating with the rest of typical NGS workflow.CLC Genomics Workbench is 
available for Windows, Mac OS X, and Linux platforms. It includes a number of features within the 
fields of genomics, transcriptomics and epigenomics, and additionally it includes all the tools of 
CLC Main Workbench. CLC Genomics Workbench supporting  all the major next generation 
sequencing platforms, such as SOLiD, Ion Torrent, Complete Genomics, 454, Illumina Genome 
Analyzer and of course also Sanger.CLC Genomics Workbench supports read mapping as well as 
de novo assembly of hybrid data. 
The second platform used for this study was MGmapper 2.2. from Center for Genomic 
epidemiology, DTU  in cooperation with Center for Biological Sequence Analysis (CBS),DTU. 
Which conducts basic research in the field of bioinformatics and systems biology. The CBS 
represents one of the large bioinformatics groups in academia in Europe and  provide the scientific 
foundation for a global web database which will allow rapid and simplified characterization of 
pathogenic, environmental and industrial bacteria. CBS  also develop statistical and epidemiological 
tools, which will allow integrated analysis of genome data with epidemiological information and 
also provide a valuable tool for rapid extraction of relevant information from whole genome 
sequences for researchers working with industrial, environmental and pathogenic bacteria.

3 Methods and Materials 
3.1 Experimental design 
 
For this study DNA extraction performed by to different extraction methods from to type of 
samples a sample-collection,  inoculated with Campylobacter jejuni : from fresh and one-year-old 
ongoing stored at -20°C samples. For purification of DNA were used QIAamp DNA Stool Mini Kit 
(Qiagen) and Easy-DNA™ Kit (Invitrogen). Total DNA outputs from samples confirmed by real-
time PCR and missered by Qubit 2.0 flurometer. DNA from five samples with the highest yield of 
total DNA were choose for Campylobacter - target enrichment by Pad Lock probe technologies. 
Next Generation Sequencing were performed in Illumina MiSeq.  The sequencing reads were 
cleaned, trimmed and analysed by MGmapper (Center for Genomic epidemiology, DTU) and CLC 
Microbial Genomics, which includes tools for microbial community analysis as well as tools for 
epidemiological typing of microbial isolates. 
3.2. Sample collection 
 
For this study fresh chicken stool samples were randomly collected on a private chicken farm 
(Bjørn de Place) and within two hours provided in the laboratory. Breeding chickens on this farm is 
a highly modernized with a high level of hygiene, which indicates on a protection of faeces samples 
from external contaminations. Approximately 170g of fresh faeces was collected in a plastic 
container with a lid and it was placed in a cold place during transport to minimize possible DNA 
degradation. Chickens were 3 days old, therefor, additional testing of the Campy-status was not 
required.  
Total amount of 170g faeces was mixed with 340 mL of 0,9% physiological salt water  by stirring 
to get a homogenized liquid mass. Afterwards 1 g faeces mix was added directly into 2mL 
Eppendorf tube. 
3.3 Artificial inoculation of samples 
 
In-house collection broiler strain Campylobacter jejuni DVI-SC181 was recovered on blood agar. 
One colony was transferred to 9 ml of Mueller Hinton broth and incubated in a microaerobic 
atmosphere for 24 h. One ml was transferred to 100 ml of fresh Mueller Hinton broth and incubated 
for additional 3 h. From this a ten-fold serial dilution row was prepared (1×10-1 – 1×10-8), and the 
number of bacterial cells was determined by duplicate plate spreading on blood agar. Each 2mL 
Eppendorf tube with 
fecal samples were  inoculated with 1×103 and 1×106 CFU/g, respectively.  Sample was srtored at -
20°C.     
 
32 
==PAGE:33==
3.4 Isolation of genomic DNA 
 
Extraction of DNA was performed by using to different kits: “Genomic DNA isolation kit” (Easy-
DNA™ Kit, Invitrogen) and QIAamp DNA Stool Mini Kit (Qiagen) 
Total DNA was extracted from stool samples with  short storage time ( less then 30 dags), with 
QIAamp DNA Stool Mini Kit (Qiagen) following the instruction of the manufacturer with the 
following modifications. Protocol which has been used is one for the human DNA analysis as has 
been showed higher output in form of DNA quantity than protocol for pathogen detection from the 
same Kit. Modifications also included heating of sample for 5 min at 75⁰C to increase the lysis of 
cells. Briefly, fecal samples were homogenized in InhibitiEX buffer and heated at 75⁰C for 5 min to 
lyse bacterial cells. When is used stool sample stored at -20⁰C, it is important that the samples do 
not thaw until InhibitEX Buffer is added, otherwise the DNA in the sample may degrade. After 
addition of InhibitEX Buffer, all following steps can be performed at room temperature. After 
removal of potential inhibitors by incubation with an InhibitEX buffer, the lysates were treated with 
proteinase K and buffer AL at 70⁰C for 10 min to remove protein and polysaccharides. DNA was 
precipitated by ethanol , applied to a column provided in the kit followed by washes with buffers 
AW1 and AW2.  DNA was finally eluted in 100µL buffer AE. 
DNA exstractions by using Easy-DNA™ Kit( Invitrogen) were proformed following  the 
manufacturer manual.  Briefly, fecal samples were homogenized 1 min in Solution A and heated at 
65⁰C for 10 min to lyse bacterial cells and  cooled at 5⁰C. For precipitation of proteins and lipids,  
Solution B was addet , followed by chlorofotm trin and centrifugation at 20.000 g for 10 min at 4°C 
to separate phases. After centrifugation, 500 µl of the upper phase transferred  into the 1 ml of 96% 
ethanol (-20°C) , mixed and  incubated on ice for 30 min. Nexst step is a DNA precipitation started 
with centrifugation with  20.000 g for 10-15 min at 4°C and discarding the ethanol. Pellet loosen 
and washed with 500 µl of 80% ethanol (-20°C) by using a pipette, followed centrifugation at 
maximum speed for 3-5 min at 4°C. After discarding af ethanol pellet was resuspended in 100 µl  
pre mixed TE-RNase(100 µl TE + 2 µl 2 mg/ml RNase pr) and tncubate at 37°C 1-2 hours. DNA . 
 Samples DNA was srtored at -20°C.     
3.5 Detection of Campylobacter by real-time PCR 
 
Real-time PCR for thermotolerant C. jejuni, C. coli and C. lari based on amplification and detection 
of a 287-bp sequence of the 16S rRNA gene as [47] was performed on a StepOnePlus Real-Time 
PCR System with  Software v.2.3(Applised Biosystem). 
The 25 µl real-time PCR mixture contained 10 µl template DNA, 2.5 µl 10×buffer for Tth DNA 
polymerase (Roche A/S, Hvidovre, Denmark), 1 U of Tth DNA polymerase (Roche A/S), 0.6 mM 
deoxynucleoside triphosphate mixture (Amersham Pharmacia Biotech, Buckinghamshire, United 
 
33 
==PAGE:34==
Kingdom), 2.5 mM MgCl2 (Applied Biosystems), 2 µl glycerol (87% pure glycerol, Merck, 
Darmstadt, Germany) and 0.2 g/L of bovine serum albumin (BSA, Roche A/S).  
Primers used for amplification were 12.5 pmol OT1559 forward primer (5’CTG CTT AAC ACA 
AGT TGA GTA GG’3) and 12.5 pmol 18-1 reverse primer (5’TTC CTT AGG TAC CGT CAG AA 
‘3) (DNA Technology, Århus, Denmark). The probes used for detection were 1.9 pmol target 
Campylobacter probe labelled with FAM reporter dye and BHQ1 quencer dye (5’(6 
FAM)CA+TCC+TCCACGCGGCG+TTGC(BHQ1)’3;Sigma Aldrich), 1.5 pmol IAC probe 
labelled with JOE reporter dye and TAMRA quencher dye (5’JOE-TTC ATG AGG ACA CCT 
GAG TTG A-TAMRA ‘3; Sigma) and 5×103 copies of IAC (124 bp, Sigma Aldrich). The cycle 
profile was as follows: initial denaturation at 95°C for 3 min, followed by 40 cycles of 95°C for 15 
s and 58°C for 60 s [47]. Fluorescence measurements were obtained online and StepOnePlus Real-
Time PCR System with  Software v.2.3(Applised Biosystem). 
PCR reaction included a positive DNA control, a negative DNA control and a non-template control 
(NTC). Samples with a threshold cycle (Ct) response below 40 where considered to be positive. 
Samples were considered negative when no Ct value was obtained, and the signal from the IAC 
probe did not exceed the mean Ct value for uninhibited samples ± 2 standard deviations. In case of 
inhibition the analysis were repeated to exclude the possibility of a false negative result. 
3.6 Quantification of DNA on Qubit 2.0 Fluoormeter 
 
Qubit 2.0 Fluorometer uses fluorescent dyes to determine the concentration of DNA in a sample by 
binding to their targets (DNA or RNA) and using DNA standards to derive the relationship between 
DNA concentration and fluorescence. 
The extracted DNA was quantified in duplicate on a Qubit 2.0 Fluorometer (Life Technologies), 
using the Qubit dsDNA High Sensitivity Assay Kit (Life Technologies) witch quantification range 
0.2–100 ng. Qubit dsDNA High Sensitivity Assay Kit include concentrated assay reagent, dilution 
buffer, and prediluted standards. Brieflly reagens were dillutet using the provided buffer, samles 
were added and concenrtetion readed using the Qubit 2.0 Fluorometer (Figure 8) [75].  
 
 
34 
==PAGE:35==
 
Figure 8. The Qubit quantitation assay workflow [75].  
 
3.7 Target enrichment: Pad Lock probes technology 
 
Design of Pad Lock probes for Campylobacter  were based on knowledge of seven housekeeping 
genes in the MLST scheme for Campylobacter jejuni: Aspartase (aspA) 477bp, Glutamine 
synthetase (glnA) 477 bp, Citrate synthase (gltA) 402 bp, Serine hydroxy methyl transferase (glyA) 
507bp, Phosphoglucomutase (pgm) 498 bp, Transketolase (tkt) 549 bp , ATP synthase a-subunit 
(uncA) 489 bp and Hippuricase gene (hipO). The nucleotide sequences of these seven loci form an 
allelic profile of a strain, which indicate its genotype. Each unique allele has been assign a number 
in order of discovery, and any combination of the seven numbers make up a sequence type (ST) 
[58].  
Construction of cLPPs (complementary long Pad Lock probes) is a PCR-based method with  
enzymatic synthesis of Pad Lock probes in the range of a few hundred nucleotides, labeled by 
incorporation of modified nucleotides during synthesis. 
First producing of cLPP (complementary Long Pad Lock Probe) towards the 7 Campylobacter 
MLST genes + HipO were performing by PCRs with LPP1+LPP2 and LPP3+LPP4 on pUC57-
LPP299 plasmid (containing a 299bp random fragment). Primers for this step were designed and 
produced by DNA-Technology and specifics for each reaction (Table 5). 
Each PCR reaction in 50µl volume contained 25,5µl of Nuclease-free water, 10 µl Apta buffer, 1µl 
10 mM dNTPs, 5µl MgCl2, 1µl Apta pol, 2,5µl pUC57-LPP299 (1ng/µl), 2,5µl 10 µM Forward 
Primer and 2,5µl10 µM Reverse Primer. There were proformed 14 reactions with different 
primercombination ( Table z) with followed termoprofile: 1cykel 30 seconds at 95°C, 30 cykle 10 
seconds at 95°C 40 seconds at 60°C, 1cykle 2 min at 72°C.

Next step to digest with MlyI and BsaI restrictions enzymes to remove restriction sites and permit 
proper annealing to the Camp MLST targets. One unit is defined as the amount of enzyme required 
to digest 1 µg of  DNA in 1 hour at 37°C in a total reaction volume of 50 µl [76].  
 Each reaction was performed in a 50µl total volume contaning 20µl MLST cLPP raw PCR reaction 
from previous step, 2µl MlyI 10U/µl (NEB) , 1µl BsaI HF 20U/µl (NEB),3µl 10X CutSmart buffer 
and 24µl H2O.Digesting were for for 2 hour at 37°C , followed heat inactivate restriction enzymes 
for 20min at 65°C. and column purification in 40µl. 
Probs LPP1+LPP2 are pooled in tube named cLPP1s and LPP3+LPP4 are pooled in tube named 
cLPP3 , column purified and eluted in 50 µl H20 for further use.  
Results confirmed by agarose gel run, where MLST probs have a size of approximately 300-350 bp.     
The third step Camp MLST cLPPs have been tested for their ability to capture the MLST DNA 
fragments.  This step required followed materials: cLPP probes from previous step, Campylobacter 
purified  DNA, 1 M betaine (Sigma),dNTP (GE), 10mM each, Ampligase buffer (Epicentre), 
Ampligase (Epicentre), Phusion polymerase (ThermoScientific),ExoI (ThermoScientific), ExoIII 
(ThermoScientific), Lambda Exo (ThermoScientific),H20. 
 
Hybridization mix in total volume 15µl contained 2µl Campylobacter purified   DNA, 1µl C. 
cLPP1s 299 (concentration 50 ng/µl) or C. cLPP3s 299 (concentration 70 ng/µl), 1,5µl Ampligase 
buffer, 2µl Betaine (5M), 8,5µl H2O. 24 tubes were heated to 98°C for 3min, then decrease the 
temperature decreased by 2,4C/min (Ramp 1%) to 88, 78, 68, 63 and then 58°C for 2 hour. 
 
Probe extension and ligation mix in total volume 5µl for each reaction contained 0,4µl dNTP (10 
mM each), 2µl Betaine (5M), 0,4µl NAD+ (50mM), 0,5µl Ampligase buffer, 1µl Ampligase 
(5U/µl), 0,25µl Phusion pol. (2U/µl), 0,45µl H2O. 
The 5 µl probe extension-ligation mix was added to each of 24 the hybridization mixes and heated 
to 58°C for 1hour, followed by 20min at 68°C. 
 
Exonuclease mix in total volume 20µl for each reaction contained 1µl ExoI (20U/µl), 0,5µl ExoIII 
(200U/µl),  0,5 Lambda exo (1000U/µl NO à true = 10U/µl), 2µl Lambda exo buffer (10x) and 16µl 
H2O , was premixed and addet to preheated to 94°C and colled to 50°C samles. Heating continued  
with 37°C for 40 min followed  80°C I 10 min.
